Objection and Answer on the Final Cause
Objection and answer on the final cause distinguishing mental vs external causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the final cause's causality is distinguished between mental and external existence to resolve an apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
The final cause works in the mind, not in external existence.

Sanusi presents an objection to the claim that the final cause of an action is the actual external thing (like sitting). The objection seems to show a contradiction. But the answer clarifies by distinguishing between the thing itself and its conceptual causality. This lesson walks through the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the objection and the answer structure.
The passage is a compact objection-answer pair.
2
Identify the two parts of the objection
The objection first targets the interpretation that the final cause is the actual sitting, then targets the interpretation that it is the conception of sitting.
The objection uses the example of a bed and sitting.
3
Understand the answer's distinction
The answer affirms that the final cause is the sitting itself, but its causality is in the mind (by conception), not in external existence.
This resolves the objection because the effect depends on the mental conception of the final cause.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the final cause operates in the mind rather than externally. This highlights the role of intention and purpose in causation.
This distinction is crucial in kalam discussions of causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وإما ما لأجله الشيء كالجلوس إلخ) ظاهر كلامه يدل على أن العلة الغائية نفس الجلوس. فإن قلت: المقرر انتفاء المعلول بانتفاء جزء من علته التامة، مع عدم انتفاء السرير بانتفاء نفس الجلوس، وإن اعتبر العلة الغائية تصور الجلوس يرد عليه أن الغاية معلولة في الخارج كما صرح به ولا يستقيم هذا في نفس التصور. قلت: العلة الغائية نفس الجلوس، لكن عليته في الذهن أي باعتبار تصوره، ويلزم من انتفاء الجلوس بهذا الاعتبار انتفاء المعلول؛ إذ مآل المعنى حينئذ انتفاء تصورها.
His saying: (And that for which the thing exists like sitting etc.) The apparent meaning of his speech indicates that the final cause is the sitting itself. For if you say: It is established that the effect ceases to exist if a part of its complete cause ceases, while the bed does not cease to exist if the sitting itself ceases, and if the final cause is considered to be the conception of sitting, then it is objected that the end is caused in external existence as he stated, and this is not consistent in the conception itself. I say: The final cause is the sitting itself, but its causality is in the mind, i.e., by virtue of its conception, and from the cessation of sitting in this respect follows the cessation of the effect; because the meaning then is the cessation of its conception.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer pair concerning the final cause. The objection is that if the final cause is the actual sitting (as the example suggests), then the bed should cease to exist when sitting ceases, but it does not. If it is said that the final cause is the conception of sitting, then the objection is that the end (ghaya) is itself an effect in external existence, so it cannot be merely a conception. The answer distinguishes two aspects: the final cause is indeed the sitting itself, but its causality (illiyya) is in the mind, i.e., it is considered as a conception. When the sitting ceases to be conceived (i.e., when the purpose is no longer intended), then the bed ceases (i.e., becomes useless). Thus, the objection is resolved by noting that the final cause operates in the mental order, not the external order.

Key Terms
العلة الغائية
الذهن
الخارج
التصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the final cause must be external and actual for it to be a cause.
Correction: The final cause's causality is in the mind, not in external existence. Its external reality is not what causes the effect; rather, the conception of it as a purpose drives the causation.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, in what respect does the final cause have causality?
A) In external existence
B) In the mind (conception)
C) In both equally
D) In neither

Key Takeaway
The final cause is the thing itself, but its causal role is in the mind, not in external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you set a purpose for your actions—like studying to gain knowledge—do you treat that purpose as something real only when it is achieved externally, or do you recognize its power in your mind even before it is realized? How does this distinction affect your perseverance when the external outcome is delayed?
?
Have you ever abandoned a task because the mental image of its purpose faded, even though the external circumstances remained unchanged? What does that reveal about the real cause that moves you—is it the external thing or your conception of it?
?
If the final cause works in the mind, then your intentions are not just passive thoughts but actual causes of your actions. Knowing this, how will you guard your mental purposes today against negligence or corruption? What specific purpose will you consciously hold in your mind to guide your next action?

The Four Causes and the Essence-Existence Distinction
Al-Jurjani classifies the four causes and distinguishes cause of essence from cause of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four causes according to al-Jurjani and why matter and form are called causes of essence while agent and end are called causes of existence.

Core Idea
Matter and form cause both essence and existence; agent and end cause existence only.

Al-Jurjani begins by asserting that the need of one thing for another is a necessary conception. He then divides causes into two pairs: internal (matter and form) and external (agent and end). The internal causes are part of the thing’s constitution, so they are causes of its essence; the external causes bring the thing into being and are causes of its existence. This passage lays the foundational vocabulary for Islamic philosophical discussions of causality.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the necessity of conceiving need
Note that al-Jurjani begins by stating that the conception of a thing's need for another is necessary. This sets the context that causality is a fundamental concept.
This is a brief preamble.
2
Identify the four causes
Read the passage and list the four causes mentioned: matter, form, agent, and end. Notice that matter and form are internal to the effect, while agent and end are external.
3
Distinguish cause of essence from cause of existence
The passage states that matter and form are called 'cause of essence' because they enter into the constitution of the thing, but they also cause existence. Agent and end are only causes of existence, not of essence.
4
Consider the restriction to composite things
Al-Jurjani notes that matter and form exist only for composite things. Think about what this implies for simple entities like God or immaterial substances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الأول : تصور احتياج الشيء إلى غيره ضروري. وهاتان أي الصورة والمادة علتان للماهية داخلتان في قوامها كما أنها علتان للوجود أيضاً، لتوقفه عليهما. فيخصان باسم علة الماهية تمييزاً لهما عن الباقيتين المشاركتين إياهما في علية الوجود. والثاني: أعني ما يكون خارجاً عن المعلول، إما ما به الشيء كالنجار له أي للسرير وهو الفاعل والمؤثر، وإما ما لأجله الشيء كالجلوس عليه له وهو الغاية أي العلة الغائية. وهاتان العلتان أعني الفاعل والغاية يخصان باسم علة الوجود؛ لتوقفه عليهما دون الماهية. والأوليان وهما المادة والصورة لا توجدان إلا للمركب.
The Fifth Observation - First Objective: The conception of a thing's need for another is necessary. These two, i.e., form and matter, are causes of the essence, entering into its constitution, and they are also causes of existence because [existence] depends on them. They are specifically called 'cause of essence' to distinguish them from the other two that share with them in causality of existence. The second [type]—i.e., what is external to the effect—is either that by which the thing [exists], like the carpenter for the bed, which is the agent and the efficient cause; or that for which the thing [exists], like sitting on it, which is the end, i.e., the final cause. These two causes—namely, the agent and the end—are specifically called 'cause of existence' because [existence] depends on them, not the essence. The first two—namely, matter and form—exist only for composite things.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage classifies causes into two categories: internal (matter and form) and external (agent and end). It then distinguishes between cause of essence (ʿillat al-māhiyyah) and cause of existence (ʿillat al-wujūd). The internal causes are causes of both essence and existence, while the external causes are causes of existence only. The passage ends by noting that matter and form exist only for composite things.

Key Terms
الماهية
الوجود
المادة
الصورة
الفاعل
الغاية
علة الماهية
علة الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Internal causes (matter and form) are only causes of essence, not of existence.
Correction: Al-Jurjani explicitly states that matter and form are causes of both essence and existence, but they are specifically called 'cause of essence' to distinguish them from the external causes that share in causality of existence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, which two causes are specifically called 'cause of existence' (علة الوجود)?
A) Matter and form
B) Agent and end
C) Matter and agent
D) Form and end

Key Takeaway
Matter and form are causes of both essence and existence, while agent and end are causes of existence only.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says matter and form only exist for composite things. You are a composite of body and soul: how does your awareness of your own composition change your understanding of your dependence on the Creator for your essence and existence?
?
Al-Jurjani states that the conception of a thing's need for another is necessary. Why do you often live as if you are self-sufficient? What would change if you truly internalized your need for the ultimate Cause?

The Final Cause Belongs Only to Free Agents
al-Jurjani distinguishes free and necessitated agents regarding final causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the final cause (al-ghāyah) applies exclusively to agents of free choice and not to necessitated agents, and what it means to analogously call a benefit a 'ghāyah'.

Core Idea
The final cause is only meaningful for free agents who act for purposes.

This lesson examines al-Jurjani's key distinction: the final cause (ghāyah) is exclusive to agents who act by free choice. A necessitated agent (mujib) does not act for a purpose, though its action may have wisdom or benefit that is analogously called a ghāyah. Understanding this clarifies the scope of teleological explanations in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the core claim that the final cause is only for free agents.
Pay attention to the contrast between free and necessitated agent.
2
Identify the two types of agents
Notice how al-Jurjani distinguishes between the free agent (mukhtār) and the necessitated agent (mujib). The former acts with purpose, the latter without.
3
Understand the analogy
Consider why the benefit of a necessitated agent's act is called 'ghāyah' only by analogy to the true final cause.
This clarifies that not every beneficial outcome is a genuine final cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والغاية لا تكون إلا لفاعل بالاختيار، مراده أن العلة الغائية لا تكون إلا للمختار، لا فإن الموجب لا يكون لفعله علة غائية، وإن جاز أن يكون لفعله حكمة وفائدة، فائدة فعل الموجب غاية أيضاً تشبيهاً لها بالغاية الحقيقية التي هي علة غائية للفعل، وغرض مقصود للفاعل. والغاية معلولة في الخارج وإن كانت علة في الذهن فإن الجلوس على السرير مثلا معلول بحسب الخارج لوجود السرير، وعلة له بحسب تصوره وحصوله في الذهن، فلها للغاية علاقتا العلية والمعلولية بالقياس إلى شيء واحد، لكن باعتبار وجوديها الذهني والخارجي.
The end (al-ghayah) exists only for an agent of free choice (fāʻil bi-al-ikhtiyār). His intended meaning is that the final cause exists only for the free agent; indeed, the necessitated agent (al-mujib) does not have a final cause for his act, although it is possible that his act has wisdom and benefit. The benefit of the necessitated agent's act is also called a 'ghāyah' by analogy to the true ghāyah, which is a final cause for the act and an intended purpose for the agent. The ghāyah is an effect in external existence even though it is a cause in the mind; for example, sitting on the bed is an effect externally due to the existence of the bed, and it is a cause of the bed according to its conceptualization and existence in the mind. Thus, the ghāyah has two relationships: causality and effect in relation to one thing, but according to its two modes of existence: mental and external.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage clarifies the nature of the final cause (al-ghāyah). It first states that a final cause exists only for an agent who acts by free choice (mukhtār), not for a necessitated agent (mujib). The reason is that a necessitated agent acts without a purpose; its action is necessitated by its nature. However, the benefit or wisdom of its action may be analogously called a ghāyah. The passage then explains that the ghāyah is an effect in external reality—e.g., sitting on the bed is an effect of the bed's existence—but it is a cause in the mind because the agent's conception of the purpose leads to the action. Thus the ghāyah has a dual relationship: as a cause in the mind and an effect in reality.

Key Terms
الغاية
العلة الغائية
الفاعل بالاختيار
الموجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any beneficial outcome is a final cause for the agent that produced it.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only free agents have final causes; necessitated agents do not, even if their actions have benefits.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, which type of agent can have a final cause for its action?
A) Necessitated agent (mujib)
B) Free agent (mukhtār)
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The final cause is a concept that only applies to free agents who act for an intended purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily actions, do you act with conscious intention, like a free agent, or do you often go through the motions without purpose? What would change if you truly intended every action for a noble goal?
?
Consider a good deed you did recently. Was its benefit truly intended by you, or was it just a byproduct? How can you ensure your actions become true final causes with sincere intentions?
?
The lesson tells us that even a necessitated agent's act can have wisdom, but it is not a real purpose. Are there areas in your life where you assume that because something is beneficial, you must be doing it for the right reason? Examine your intentions today.

The Dual Relationship of the Final Cause: Cause in Mind, Effect in Reality
al-Jurjani explains the dual causality of the final cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the final cause (al-ghāyah) functions as a cause in the mind but an effect in external reality.

Core Idea
The final cause is both a cause (in the mind) and an effect (in reality).

Al-Jurjani now explains a subtle yet crucial point: the final cause has a dual nature. It is a cause in the mind because the agent's conception of the purpose leads to the action, but in the external world, the final cause is an effect of other causes. The example of sitting on a bed illustrates this clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and example
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the example of sitting on the bed.
2
Analyze the dual relationship
Understand how the same thing (sitting) is both an effect (of the bed's existence) and a cause (of the bed's conceptualization).
The ghāyah is an effect externally but a cause in the mind.
3
Connect to the bigger picture
Reflect on why this dual relationship matters for theological causality – it shows that final causality involves both intention and external conditions.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage shows that the same thing (sitting) can be both cause and effect depending on perspective. Is there a situation in your life where you have treated something as only an effect (something that happens to you) when in fact you are also a cause of it by your mental attitude? How might acknowledging your role as a 'cause in the mind' change your response?
?
If your intentions (causes in the mind) lead to real effects (external actions), how accountable are you for the states of your heart? Do you tend to focus more on external results or on the mental causes? What is one intention you need to examine and purify today, knowing it has a dual nature?

The Complete Cause and the Question of Its Composition
al-Jurjani defines the sufficient cause and discusses whether it must be composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the complete cause and why the suggestion of composition in its definition does not imply necessity.

Core Idea
The complete cause is everything a thing needs—but it need not be composite.

al-Jurjani opens by defining the complete cause as everything a thing requires for its essence and existence, or for existence alone. The word 'everything' hints at composition, but he immediately clarifies that composition is not necessary. This lesson unpacks that definition and the ensuing debate about whether the cause must have parts, drawing on Ashʿarī views on existence and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and the initial problem
Carefully read the first part of the passage: the definition of the complete cause and the observation that 'everything' suggests composition. Notice that the author says composition is not necessary.
The term 'complete cause' (ʿillah tāmmah) is central.
2
Follow the objection and responses
The passage then presents an objection: if the cause has essence and existence, that would imply composition. The response argues that existence may be identical to essence, and absolute existence is not required. Trace how the discussion moves through Ashʿarī commitments.
Pay attention to the role of 'absolute existence' (al-wujūd al-muṭlaq).
3
Understand the necessity objection and Taftāzānī's answer
A further objection claims that necessity (wujūb) would be part of the cause, leading to composition. The response shows that would entail circularity (priority of necessity over itself). Taftāzānī's answer is that necessity is an effect, not a part, of the complete cause.
This resolves the difficulty without requiring composition.
4
Grasp the commentator's purpose
The final lines explain that the definition's phrase 'everything' is loose but not problematic, because the principle does not require composition. Recognize the overall aim: to defend the definition while acknowledging its verbal imperfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسمى جميع ما يحتاج إليه الشيء في ماهيته ووجوده أو في وجوده فقط علة تامة. وفي لفظ الجميع نوع إشعار بوجوب التركيب في العلة التامة، وذلك غير واجب.
قوله : ( وفي لفظ الجميع نوع إشعار إلخ ) إنما قال : نوع إشعار إيماء إلى إمكان توجيهه بأن المراد أن لا يبقى شيء يحتاج إليه لا أن تكون مركبة إليه . قوله: (وذلك غير واجب ألا ترى إلخ قال : قلت : للعلة ماهية ووجود، وكل منهما محتاج إليه فيلزم التركيب، ولو اعتبر وجوده الخاص عين ماهيته فلا شك في زيادة الوجود المطلق . قلت : زيادة الوجود المطلق بحسب الواقع لا يستدعي احتياج المعلول إلى وجود مطلق زائد على ذات العلة، كيف ولا وجود مطلقاً عند الشيخ الأشعري ومتابعيه في ذلك؟ والوجود العلة الخاص عين مع تمام وجود المعلول فليتأمل. فإن قلت كل ممكن مسبوق وجوده بوجود به كما تقرر عندهم. فحينئذ يكون الوجوب من جملة الموقوف عليه فيلزم التركيب . قلت : وجوب كون الوجوب السابق على تقدير تحققه جزءاً من العلة التامة وإن أشار إليه صاحب التنقيح، وأنكر بهذا سبق الوجوب، غير صحيح؛ للزوم تقدمه على نفسه؛ لأنهم صرحوا بكونه أثر العلة التامة متأخراً عنها مع لزوم تقدمه عليها، على تقدير كونه جزءاً منها، وهو محال، لكن أشار الفاضل التفتازاني إلى جوابه بأن الوجوب عندهم يؤكد الوجود، فلم يعتبروه جزءاً من العلة التامة، بل اعتبروه أثرا لها ومقصود الشارح هو التنبيه على أن في تعريفهم مسامحة لإشعار لفظ الجميع بوجوب التركيب مع عدم وجوبه عندهم بمقتضى قاعدتهم فلا إشكال.
Everything that a thing needs in its essence and existence, or in its existence only, is called a 'complete cause' (ʿillah tāmmah). In the expression 'everything' (al-jamīʿ) there is a kind of suggestion that composition is necessary in the complete cause, but that is not necessary.
His statement: 'And in the expression "everything" there is a kind of suggestion, etc.' He only said 'kind of suggestion' as an indication of the possibility of interpreting it to mean that no thing is needed, not that it is composed of parts. His statement: 'and that is not necessary, do you not see, etc.' He said: I say: The cause has an essence and an existence, and each of them is needed, so composition follows. If one considers its specific existence to be identical to its essence, there is no doubt about the increase of absolute existence. I say: The increase of absolute existence according to actual reality does not require that the effect need an absolute existence additional to the cause's essence—how indeed if there is no absolute existence according to al-Shaykh al-Ashʿarī and his followers in that? The specific existence of the cause is identical with the complete existence of the effect, so let one reflect. If you say: Every contingent is preceded in its existence by an existence due to it, as established with them; then necessity (wujūb) would be among the things on which it depends, and composition would follow. I say: The necessity of the [thing] that the prior necessity, on the assumption of its realization, is part of the complete cause—although the author of al-Tanqīḥ alluded to it and denied thereby the priority of necessity—is incorrect because it would entail its being prior to itself. For they stated that it is an effect of the complete cause, posterior to it, while it would have to be prior to it on the assumption that it is part of it, which is impossible. However, al-Fāḍil al-Taftāzānī pointed to an answer: that necessity, according to them, emphasizes existence, so they did not consider it part of the complete cause, but rather considered it an effect of it. The commentator's purpose is to point out that in their definition there is a looseness due to the suggestion of the word 'everything' that composition is necessary, while it is not necessary according to their own principle, so there is no difficulty.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage defines the complete cause (al-ʿillah al-tāmmah) as everything a thing needs for its essence and existence, or for existence alone. The expression 'everything' suggests composition, but the author argues that composition is not necessary. The commentary debates this: if the cause has essence and existence, that would imply composition; but if existence is identical to essence, then absolute existence is additional. The discussion involves Ashʿarī views on existence and necessity, and concludes with Taftāzānī's response that necessity (wujūb) is not part of the complete cause but an effect of it, so composition is not required.

التركيب
الوجود المطلق
الوجوب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The complete cause must always be composed of multiple parts because the definition uses 'everything'.
Correction: The word 'everything' only means all that is needed, not that the cause is composite. The author argues composition is not necessary.

Checkpoint

A) Because the word 'everything' can be interpreted as covering all needed items without implying parts
B) Because the complete cause is always simple
C) Because the definition is only about existence, not essence
D) Because the Ashʿarīs deny absolute existence

Key Takeaway
The complete cause is defined as everything needed, but composition is not a necessary feature of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage shows that necessity is not part of the cause but an effect of it. In your practice of faith, do you sometimes treat Allah's commands as if they are composite necessities that you must fulfill in a particular way? How can you submit to His simple will without adding your own conditions?
?
Al-Jurjani warns against reading composition into the complete cause. Is there an area of your belief where you have unintentionally added 'parts' to Allah's oneness—perhaps by overemphasizing attributes as separate? What step will you take today to purify your understanding of tawhid?

The Necessitating Agent and the Final Cause
Explains absence of true final cause for necessitating agent and dual nature of final cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessitating agent does not have a true final cause for its act, how its benefit is called a final cause by analogy, and the dual relation of the final cause as cause in the mind and effect in the external world.

Core Idea
The final cause is an effect externally but a cause in the mind.

This lesson explores the relationship between the necessitating agent and the final cause. The necessitating agent acts by necessity without aiming at a goal, yet its act may produce benefit, which is called a final cause by analogy. Moreover, the final cause has a fascinating dual nature: it is an effect in the external world but a cause in the intellect. Understanding this clarifies foundational causality concepts in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish necessitating agent from free agent
The passage begins by stating that the necessitating agent has no final cause. Reflect on why a necessitating agent acts by necessity and cannot have an intention, unlike a free agent.
Free agents can have a final cause.
2
Understand the analogy of benefit
Even though the act has no true final cause, its benefit is called a final cause by analogy because it resembles a purpose that follows the act.
3
Grasp the dual relation of the final cause
The final cause is an effect in the external world (caused by the act) but a cause in the mind (its conception motivates the agent). The example of sitting on a chair illustrates this.
In the mind, the final cause precedes the act; in reality, it follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الأول : تصور احتياج الشيء إلى غيره ضروري
فإن الموجب لا يكون لفعله علة غائية، وإن جاز أن يكون لفعله حكمة وفائدة، فائدة فعل الموجب غاية أيضاً تشبيهاً لها بالغاية الحقيقية التي هي علة غائية للفعل، وغرض مقصود للفاعل . ( والغاية معلولة في الخارج وإن كانت علة في الذهن فإن الجلوس على السرير مثلا معلول بحسب الخارج لوجود السرير، وعلة له بحسب تصوره وحصوله في الذهن ( فلها ) أي للغاية علاقتا العلية والمعلولية بالقياس إلى شيء واحد، لكن باعتبار وجوديها الذهني والخارجي. ) ( ويسمى جميع ما يحتاج إليه الشيء ) في ماهيته ووجوده أو في وجوده فقط ( علة تامة ) وفي لفظ الجميع نوع إشعار بوجوب التركيب في العلة التامة، وذلك غير واجب
أمر غيره .
قوله : ( تشبيهاً إلخ من حيث ترتب كل منهما على الفعل.
قوله : (بحسب تصوره وحصوله في الذهن من حيث ترتبه على المعلول.
قوله : ( أو في وجوده فقط كما في المعلول البسيط .
The Fifth Observatory - First Objective: The conception of a thing's need for something else is necessary. For the necessitating agent has no final cause for its act, though it is permissible that its act have wisdom and benefit. The benefit of the necessitating agent's act is also a final cause, by way of analogy to the true final cause which is a final cause for the act and an intended purpose for the agent. (And the final cause is effect in the external world while it is a cause in the mind. For example, sitting on a chair is an effect in relation to the external world due to the existence of the chair, and a cause for it in terms of its conception and obtaining in the mind. So it—i.e., the final cause—has two relations of causality and effectness with respect to one thing, but by consideration of its mental and external existences.) (And all that a thing needs) in its essence and existence, or in its existence only, (is called a complete cause). And in the word 'all' there is a kind of suggestion of the necessity of composition in the complete cause, but that is not necessary. Something else... His words: '(by way of analogy etc.)' in terms of the ordering of each of them upon the act. His words: '(in terms of its conception and obtaining in the mind)' in terms of its ordering upon the effect. His words: '(or in its existence only)' as in the simple effect.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of a thing's need for something else and introduces the complete cause. It begins by stating that the conception of a thing's need for another is necessary. Then it discusses the final cause (غاية) in relation to the necessitating agent (موجب): such an agent does not have a true final cause for its act, but its act may have wisdom or benefit, and that benefit is called a final cause by analogy. The final cause has a dual relationship: in the external world it is an effect (since it follows the act), but in the mind it is a cause (since its conception prompts the act). The example of sitting on a chair illustrates this: the sitting is an effect of the chair's existence externally, but a cause for the chair's conception mentally. The passage then gives the definition of the complete cause (علة تامة): it is everything a thing needs in its essence and existence, or in its existence alone. The phrase 'all that it needs' suggests that the complete cause must be composed of multiple parts, but the author immediately clarifies that composition is not obligatory. The last lines are glosses explaining the phrase 'by way of analogy' (referring to the ordering of cause and effect) and the phrase 'in terms of its conception' (also referring to ordering). Another gloss notes that 'in its existence only' applies to a simple effect.

علة غائية
غاية
مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that every agent must act for a purpose, so a necessitating agent also has a final cause.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the necessitating agent has no final cause for its act, though its act may have benefit called final cause by analogy.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the final cause (غاية) in the external world?
A) It is a cause.
B) It is an effect.
C) It is both cause and effect.
D) It is neither cause nor effect.

Key Takeaway
The final cause is paradoxical: it is an effect in reality but a cause in the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you act more like a necessitating agent, going through religious duties mechanically without real intention, or like a free agent with conscious purpose? What is one prayer or ritual you have been performing without a final cause in your heart, and how will you renew your intention starting today?
?
The final cause is an effect externally but a cause in the mind. In your life, are your outward deeds — your charity, your prayers — actually flowing from a genuine inner purpose, or are they just empty motions? What specific step will you take tonight to ensure your actions reflect your mental intentions?
?
If even a necessitating agent's act can have wisdom and benefit without intention, how much more should you, as a free agent, seek to align your actions with true purpose? What is one habit that benefits you but lacks a sincere intention for Allah, and how will you transform it into an act of worship that seeks His pleasure?

The Complete Cause: Definition and Clarification
Defines the complete cause and addresses misconception about composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the complete cause (العلة التامة) as everything a thing needs in its essence and existence, or in its existence only, and why composition is not necessary.

Core Idea
A complete cause is whatever a thing needs, but it need not be composite.

This lesson focuses on the formal definition of the complete cause, a central concept in Islamic causality. The 'complete cause' refers to all prerequisites a thing requires. However, a common misconception is that 'all' implies multiple components. The passage clarifies that this is not necessarily the case, especially for simple effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the complete cause
The passage says: 'And all that a thing needs in its essence and existence, or in its existence only, is called a complete cause.' Note the two scenarios: needs in essence and existence, or just in existence.
2
Examine the phrase 'all that it needs'
The word 'all' (جميع) might suggest composition, but the author immediately points out that this is not obligatory.
The simple effect does not require multiple causes.
3
Consider the gloss on 'in its existence only'
The gloss clarifies that this applies to the simple effect, which has no essence composition.

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الأول : تصور احتياج الشيء إلى غيره ضروري
فإن الموجب لا يكون لفعله علة غائية، وإن جاز أن يكون لفعله حكمة وفائدة، فائدة فعل الموجب غاية أيضاً تشبيهاً لها بالغاية الحقيقية التي هي علة غائية للفعل، وغرض مقصود للفاعل . ( والغاية معلولة في الخارج وإن كانت علة في الذهن فإن الجلوس على السرير مثلا معلول بحسب الخارج لوجود السرير، وعلة له بحسب تصوره وحصوله في الذهن ( فلها ) أي للغاية علاقتا العلية والمعلولية بالقياس إلى شيء واحد، لكن باعتبار وجوديها الذهني والخارجي. ) ( ويسمى جميع ما يحتاج إليه الشيء ) في ماهيته ووجوده أو في وجوده فقط ( علة تامة ) وفي لفظ الجميع نوع إشعار بوجوب التركيب في العلة التامة، وذلك غير واجب

قوله : ( تشبيهاً إلخ من حيث ترتب كل منهما على الفعل.
قوله : (بحسب تصوره وحصوله في الذهن من حيث ترتبه على المعلول.
قوله : ( أو في وجوده فقط كما في المعلول البسيط .
The Fifth Observatory - First Objective: The conception of a thing's need for something else is necessary. For the necessitating agent has no final cause for its act, though it is permissible that its act have wisdom and benefit. The benefit of the necessitating agent's act is also a final cause, by way of analogy to the true final cause which is a final cause for the act and an intended purpose for the agent. (And the final cause is effect in the external world while it is a cause in the mind. For example, sitting on a chair is an effect in relation to the external world due to the existence of the chair, and a cause for it in terms of its conception and obtaining in the mind. So it—i.e., the final cause—has two relations of causality and effectness with respect to one thing, but by consideration of its mental and external existences.) (And all that a thing needs) in its essence and existence, or in its existence only, (is called a complete cause). And in the word 'all' there is a kind of suggestion of the necessity of composition in the complete cause, but that is not necessary. Something else... His words: '(by way of analogy etc.)' in terms of the ordering of each of them upon the act. His words: '(in terms of its conception and obtaining in the mind)' in terms of its ordering upon the effect. His words: '(or in its existence only)' as in the simple effect.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of a thing's need for something else and introduces the complete cause. It begins by stating that the conception of a thing's need for another is necessary. Then it discusses the final cause (غاية) in relation to the necessitating agent (موجب): such an agent does not have a true final cause for its act, but its act may have wisdom or benefit, and that benefit is called a final cause by analogy. The final cause has a dual relationship: in the external world it is an effect (since it follows the act), but in the mind it is a cause (since its conception prompts the act). The example of sitting on a chair illustrates this: the sitting is an effect of the chair's existence externally, but a cause for the chair's conception mentally. The passage then gives the definition of the complete cause (علة تامة): it is everything a thing needs in its essence and existence, or in its existence alone. The phrase 'all that it needs' suggests that the complete cause must be composed of multiple parts, but the author immediately clarifies that composition is not obligatory. The last lines are glosses explaining the phrase 'by way of analogy' (referring to the ordering of cause and effect) and the phrase 'in terms of its conception' (also referring to ordering). Another gloss notes that 'in its existence only' applies to a simple effect.

وجود
معلول بسيط

Misconception: Some think the complete cause must always be a set of multiple components.
Correction: The passage explicitly says that the suggestion of composition is not necessary; a simple effect has a non-composite complete cause.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the word 'all' in the definition of complete cause suggest?
A) That the complete cause must be simple.
B) That the complete cause must be composite.
C) That composition is not obligatory.
D) That the cause is final.

Key Takeaway
The complete cause is defined as all that a thing needs, but it may be simple or composite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Complete Cause Does Not Imply Composition
Refutes two objections: the 'all' formulation and the essence/existence argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the phrase 'all' in the definition of the complete cause does not necessitate composition, and how the Ash'ari view on existence resolves the essence/existence objection.

Core Idea
The word 'all' means nothing is missing, not that the cause is made of parts.

This lesson tackles two objections that might make you think the complete cause must be composite. First, the word 'all' seems to suggest a combination of parts. Second, the cause has both an essence and an existence, which could be seen as components. The Ash'ari response clarifies that neither objection holds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the 'all' objection
Read the first part of the Arabic (up to 'لا أن تكون مركبة إليه') and its translation. Notice how the author explains that 'a kind of suggestion' implies the interpretation is not definitive.
2
Understand the response to the 'all' objection
The commentator says the phrase is meant to convey that nothing needed is left out, not that the cause is composed of multiple parts. This is a semantic clarification.
3
Read the essence/existence objection and response
Read the section starting with 'قوله: (وذلك غير واجب)' through to 'فليتأمل'. The objection is that the cause has essence and existence, both needed, so composition follows. The response denies that absolute existence is additional in reality for Ash'aris.
4
Reflect on the Ash'ari position on existence
Consider that for Ash'aris, existence is not a real attribute added to essence. Therefore, the effect does not need an extra existence beyond the cause's particular existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (نوع إشعار إلخ إنما قال ذلك ؛ لأنه يمكن توجيهه بأن المراد به ما لا يحتاج إلى أنه يلزم العلة الغائية لكل فاعل ،مختار إذ أفعال الله تعالى غير معللة بالأغراض عند الأشاعرة . وقوله : بعد هذا، أو مع الغاية، كما في البسيط الصادر عن المختار مبني على مذهب غيرهم، أو على التجويز والاحتمال الصرف.
قوله : ( وفي لفظ الجميع نوع إشعار إلخ ) إنما قال : نوع إشعار إيماء إلى إمكان توجيهه بأن المراد أن لا يبقى شيء يحتاج إليه لا أن تكون مركبة إليه . قوله: (وذلك غير واجب ألا ترى إلخ قال : قلت : للعلة ماهية ووجود، وكل منهما محتاج إليه فيلزم التركيب، ولو اعتبر وجوده الخاص عين ماهيته فلا شك في زيادة الوجود المطلق . قلت : زيادة الوجود المطلق بحسب الواقع لا يستدعي احتياج المعلول إلى وجود مطلق زائد على ذات العلة، كيف ولا وجود مطلقاً عند الشيخ الأشعري ومتابعيه في ذلك؟ والوجود العلة الخاص عين مع تمام وجود المعلول فليتأمل. فإن قلت كل ممكن مسبوق وجوده بوجود به كما تقرر عندهم. فحينئذ يكون الوجوب من جملة الموقوف عليه فيلزم التركيب . قلت : وجوب كون الوجوب السابق على تقدير تحققه جزءاً من العلة التامة وإن أشار إليه صاحب التنقيح، وأنكر بهذا سبق الوجوب، غير صحيح؛ للزوم تقدمه على نفسه؛ لأنهم صرحوا بكونه أثر العلة التامة متأخراً عنها مع لزوم تقدمه عليها، على تقدير كونه جزءاً منها، وهو محال، لكن أشار الفاضل التفتازاني إلى جوابه بأن الوجوب عندهم يؤكد الوجود، فلم يعتبروه جزءاً من العلة التامة، بل اعتبروه أثرا لها ومقصود الشارح هو التنبيه على أن في تعريفهم مسامحة لإشعار لفظ الجميع بوجوب التركيب مع عدم وجوبه عندهم بمقتضى قاعدتهم فلا إشكال. هذا غاية ما
His words: '(a kind of suggestion etc.)' He only said that because it can be interpreted as meaning what does not require that the final cause is necessary for every agent, (since) the acts of God are not caused by purposes according to the Ash'aris. And his words after this: 'or with the final cause, as in the simple [effect] produced by the free agent' is based on the doctrine of others, or on pure permissibility and possibility. His words: '(And in the word "all" a kind of suggestion etc.)' He only said 'a kind of suggestion' as a hint towards the possibility of interpreting it to mean that nothing remains that it needs, not that it is composite. His words: '(and that is not necessary, do you not see etc.)' He said: I say: the cause has essence and existence, and each of them is needed, so composition follows. Even if its particular existence is considered identical to its essence, there is no doubt about the addition of absolute existence. I say: the addition of absolute existence in reality does not entail the effect's need for absolute existence additional to the cause's essence. How could it, when there is no absolute existence for Shaykh al-Ash'ari and his followers? And the cause's particular existence is identical with the completion of the effect's existence, so reflect. If you say: every possible is preceded in its existence by existence itself, as established by them. Then necessity would be among the things upon which it depends, so composition follows. I say: the necessity of the prior necessity, assuming its realization, being a part of the complete cause—though the author of al-Tanqih referred to it, and denied the priority of necessity—is not correct, because it would lead to its priority over itself; for they explicitly stated that it is an effect of the complete cause, posterior to it, while requiring priority over it if it were part of it, which is impossible. However, al-Fadl al-Taftazani indicated an answer: that necessity for them emphasizes existence, so they did not consider it part of the complete cause, but rather an effect of it. And the commentator's aim is to point out that their definition involves looseness, due to the word 'all' suggesting necessity of composition, while it is not necessary according to their principle, so there is no problem. This is the end of what he said.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence. It begins by explaining why the author said 'a kind of suggestion' regarding the word 'all' in the definition of complete cause. The objection is that the phrase implies composition, which would be problematic. The answer clarifies that the purpose is to include everything needed, not necessarily that the cause is composite. Then a further objection is raised: the cause consists of essence and existence, both needed, so composition follows. The response distinguishes between real and mental addition: absolute existence is not something additional in reality according to the Ash'aris, so the effect does not need an extra existence. Another objection about priority of necessity is answered by noting that necessity is an effect, not a part, of the complete cause, otherwise it would lead to circular priority. Al-Taftazani's solution is that necessity merely emphasizes existence and is not a component. The commentator concludes that the definition is loose but not problematic.

وجود مطلق

أشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The complete cause must be composite because it includes all necessary conditions.
Correction: The phrase 'all' means completeness, not composition. The cause is simple in its essence; nothing is added.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about the phrase 'all' in the definition of the complete cause?
A) It implies composition is necessary.
B) It means nothing needed is left out, not composition.
C) It refers to the final cause.
D) It is a mistake.

Key Takeaway
The complete cause's 'all' means completeness, not composition, and the Ash'ari view that existence is not a real attribute prevents composition from essence/existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If existence is not an extra attribute for God, what does that mean for your own existence? Do you see your existence as something you possess or as a gift you receive each moment? How does this truth expose any pride in you?
?
The refutation shows that completeness does not require composition. In your spiritual life, do you seek completeness by adding many practices or attachments? How can you instead trust in the simplicity of relying on God alone, as this lesson suggests?

The Priority of Necessity and the Composition Objection
Refutes the objection that the priority of necessity forces composition in the complete cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the priority of necessity does not entail composition, and how necessity is an effect rather than a part of the complete cause.

Core Idea
Necessity is an effect of the cause, not a component; otherwise it would cause circular priority.

This lesson addresses a third objection: if every possible is preceded by existence itself, then necessity would be part of the cause, leading to composition. The response shows that necessity is actually an effect, and al-Taftazani clarifies that necessity merely emphasizes existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection
Read from 'فإن قلت كل ممكن مسبوق وجوده بوجود' through 'فيلزم التركيب'. The objection is that necessity is prior to existence, so it would be part of the cause.
2
Understand the response on priority
The commentator argues that if necessity were a part, it would have to be prior to the cause itself, leading to circularity. Necessity is the effect, not a part.
3
Learn al-Taftazani's solution
Al-Taftazani says that necessity is merely an emphasis on existence; it is not a separate component. Therefore, it does not make the cause composite.
4
Reflect on the commentator's conclusion
The commentator notes that the definition is loose but not problematic, as the word 'all' suggests composition but it is not required.

Types of Complete Cause and Incomplete Cause
Classifies complete cause and defines incomplete cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classifications of complete cause and the essential priority of the incomplete cause.

Core Idea
The complete cause can be one, two, or four parts; the incomplete cause is essentially prior.

This lesson introduces the types of complete cause—efficient alone, efficient with final, or all four (or three) causes—and clarifies that the incomplete cause always has essential priority over its effect. Understanding these categories is foundational for analyzing causal relationships in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on types of complete cause
Read the Arabic passage once to identify the three types mentioned. Note the examples for each type.
The passage uses parentheses to denote quotations from the original matn.
2
Analyze each type of complete cause
List the three types: (1) efficient cause alone, (2) efficient + final cause, (3) all four or three causes together. Understand the conditions for each.
3
Examine the definition of incomplete cause
Read the definition: the incomplete cause has essential priority, and temporal priority is allowed except for the formal cause. Compare with the complete cause as a collection.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why the incomplete cause is always essentially prior but may not be temporally prior. How does this align with the complete cause being a collection?
This is a key distinction in kalam causality.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( وأما العلة التامة على تقدير تركبها من أربع أو ثلاث فمجموع أمور، كل واحد منها
·
قوله : ( ومن تتمته فكأنه قيل ما يحتاج إليه الشيء الممكن في وجوده، فلا يعتبر في جانب العلة؛ لأن ما هو معتبر في المحتاج لا يعتبر في المحتاج إليه وما أورده عليه من أن اعتباره في جانب المعلول لا يقتضي عدم اعتباره في جانب العلة كالعلة المادية والصورية، فمدفوع بأن المعلول في المركب حقيقة هو التركيب والتأليف بين المادية والصورية، كما نص عليه في الإشارات، فلا يكونان معتبرين في جانب المعلول . قيل : إنه يشكل بالتأثير، والاحتياج، والوجود المطلق الزائد على الماهية التي هو نفس الوجود الخاص، والوجود السابق. والجواب : أنه ليس شيء منها مما يحتاج إليه المعلول بل هي أمور إضافية ينتزعها العقل من استتباع وجود العلة لوجود المعلول، وحكم العقل بأنه أمكن فاحتاج فأثر فيه الفاعل. فوجب وجوده فوجد، فإنما هي في الملاحظة العقلية، وليس في الخارج إلا المعلول الممكن، أو العلة الموجبة لوجوده، فتدبر فإنه دقيق. وأما رفع المانع فإن أريد به المانع في نفس الأمر فيجوز أن لا يكون فيه مانع ، وإن أريد به المانع الفرضي فإنما يستلزم الترتيب الفرضي لا في نفس الأمر.
وجودي
يقال، فإن قلت : ارتفاع الموانع شرط وعدم تصور المانع لا يضر في التوقف فيلزم التركيب . قلت إن اعتبر ارتفاع الموانع كاشفا عن شرط وجودي فالأمر ظاهر إذ لا يتوقف المعلول على شرط أصلاً، وإلا فالتركيب على تقدير عدم تصور المانع يكون فرضياً لا حقيقياً. هذا، بقي هاهنا بحث : وهو أن المعلول كما يتوقف على ذات الفاعل يتوقف على إمكان فاعليته، وأن ماهية الممكن علة قابلة على أن اعتبار إمكان الصادر في جانب المعلول لا يمنع اعتباره في جانب العلة أيضاً. ألا يرى أن كلاً من الجزء الصوري والمادي، مع أنه جزء من المعلول، جزء من العلة التامة أيضاً. فلو كان الإمكان جزء من العلة التامة مع كونه صفة للمعلول ومعتبراً فيه لم يلزم محذور، وأيضاً لما كان الإمكان من شرائط التأثير لم يوجد مؤثر بلا اشتراط أمر في تأثيره، فليتأمل. قوله : ( والعلة الناقصة متقدمة قد نبهناك على أن مجموع المادة والصورة ليس علة ،ناقصة، وإن كان جزءاً من العلة التامة .
Do you not see his words: '(And it)' i.e., the complete cause '(may be an efficient cause)' either alone, like the necessitating agent from which a simple [effect] proceeds, when there is no condition whose existence is considered, nor an obstacle whose absence is considered. As for the possibility of the effect, it is considered on the side of the effect and part of its completion; for when we find a possible thing, we seek its cause. '(Or with the final cause, as in the simple [effect])' produced by the free agent, '(and it may be composed of the four mentioned, as in the composite [effect])' produced by the free agent, and it may be composed of three of them, as in the composite produced by the necessitating agent. '(And the incomplete cause is prior to the effect by essential priority, whether it is internal to it or external to it. As for temporal priority, it is permissible except for the formal cause, for it is simultaneous with the effect in time.)' '(And as for the complete cause, assuming it is composed of four or three, then it is a collection of things, each one of them...' 
His words: '(and from its completion)' as if it were said: what the possible thing needs in its existence, so it is not considered on the side of the cause, because what is considered in the needer is not considered in the needed. And the objection raised against him that its consideration on the side of the effect does not preclude its consideration on the side of the cause, like the material and formal causes, is refuted by saying that the effect in the composite is really the composition and arrangement between the material and formal, as stated in al-Isharat, so they are not considered on the side of the effect. It is said: it is problematic regarding efficacy, need, absolute existence additional to the essence which is the particular existence, and prior existence. The answer: none of these are what the effect needs, but they are relational matters extracted by the intellect from the entailment of the cause's existence for the effect's existence, and the intellect's judgment that it was possible, so it needed, so the agent acted upon it, so its existence became necessary, so it existed. They are only in intellectual consideration, and nothing exists externally except the possible effect or the cause necessitating its existence, so reflect, for it is subtle. As for the removal of the obstacle: if it is meant as the obstacle in actual fact, it is permissible that there be no obstacle; if it is meant as the hypothetical obstacle, then it only entails hypothetical ordering, not in actual fact. 'existential' It is said: if you say: the removal of obstacles is a condition, and the non-conception of an obstacle does not harm the dependency, so composition follows. I say: if the removal of obstacles is considered as revealing an existential condition, then the matter is clear, for the effect does not depend on any condition at all; otherwise, composition, assuming no conception of an obstacle, is hypothetical, not real. This, and there remains a discussion here: that the effect depends on the essence of the agent and also on the possibility of his agency, and that the essence of the possible is a receptive cause. Yet considering the possibility of the effect on the side of the effect does not prevent considering it on the side of the cause as well. Do you not see that each of the formal and material parts, while being part of the effect, is also part of the complete cause? So if possibility were part of the complete cause while being an attribute of the effect and considered in it, no problem would follow. Also, since possibility is among the conditions of efficacy, no agent exists without a condition for its efficacy, so reflect. His words: '(And the incomplete cause is prior) we have alerted you that the combination of matter and form is not an incomplete cause, even if it is part of the complete cause.'
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of complete cause and discusses the incomplete cause. It begins by listing three types of complete cause: (1) efficient cause alone, exemplified by a necessitating agent producing a simple effect, without any condition or obstacle; the possibility of the effect is considered on the side of the effect, not the cause. (2) Efficient cause together with final cause, as in a simple effect from a free agent. (3) All four causes together, as in a composite effect from a free agent, or three causes in a composite from a necessitating agent. Then it defines the incomplete cause (علة ناقصة): it has essential priority over the effect, whether internal or external, and temporal priority is generally permissible except for the formal cause which is simultaneous. The commentary then addresses objections: the possibility of the effect is not considered on the side of the cause because it is part of the effect's completion; however, this is nuanced because material and formal causes are on both sides. Further objections about efficacy, need, absolute existence, and prior existence are answered by saying these are intellectual abstractions, not real needs. The removal of obstacles is analyzed as either real (no obstacle) or hypothetical (only hypothetical composition). Finally, a discussion remains: the effect depends on the agent's essence and the possibility of his agency; possibility could be part of the complete cause without contradiction, and it is a condition of efficacy. The passage concludes by noting that matter and form together are not an incomplete cause, but are part of the complete cause.

Key Terms

علة فاعلية
علة غائية
علة ناقصة
موجب
مختار
بسيط
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the incomplete cause is always temporally prior to the effect.
Correction: The passage states that temporal priority is permissible except for the formal cause; essential priority is what defines the incomplete cause.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of complete cause involves only the efficient cause?
A) When a free agent produces a simple effect.
B) When a necessitating agent produces a simple effect without conditions or obstacles.
C) When a composite effect is produced by a free agent.
D) When a composite effect is produced by a necessitating agent.

Key Takeaway
The complete cause may consist of one, two, or four (or three) partial causes, while the incomplete cause always has essential priority over its effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on a cause in your life (e.g., effort, medicine, relationships), do you ever consider that Allah may be the complete cause acting alone without any conditions? How does this realization change your trust in Him?
?
The passage distinguishes between a necessitating agent (acting by necessity) and a free agent (acting by will). Which of these describes how you perceive Allah's actions in your life? Will you commit to seeing His actions as free and purposeful, even when you don't understand the purpose?
?
If the incomplete cause is essentially prior but not always temporally prior, what does that say about your impatient demands for immediate results? Are you willing to wait for Allah's wisdom in the timing of causes and effects, even when the delay tests your faith?

Causality and Created Things

Nuances in Causality: Possibility and Obstacles
Addresses objections to causal classification and refines concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections regarding the possibility of the effect, removal of obstacles, and whether possibility can be part of the complete cause.

Core Idea
The possibility of an effect is considered on the effect's side, not the cause's; objections are resolved by distinguishing real from hypothetical.

This lesson explores philosophical objections to the earlier classification, focusing on the role of possibility, the removal of obstacles, and whether possibility can be part of the complete cause. These subtle discussions refine our understanding of causality and address common misconceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections and responses
Read the Arabic passage carefully. The passage presents several objections and their answers. Note the structure: objection then response.
The passage uses 'قيل' for objections and 'الجواب' for answers.
2
Understand the first objection about possibility
The first objection argues that possibility should be considered on the cause side, but the author responds that it is part of the effect's completion.
The response uses the example of material and formal causes to argue that they are on both sides.
3
Analyze the discussion on removal of obstacles
The passage distinguishes between real obstacles and hypothetical obstacles. Removal of obstacles is not a condition for the cause but a hypothetical ordering.
The term 'رفع المانع' is key.
4
Evaluate the final discussion on possibility as part of complete cause
The passage concludes that possibility could be part of the complete cause without contradiction, as it is a condition of efficacy.
This is a subtle point that reconciles earlier statements.

Causality and Created Things
beginner

The Complete Cause: Its Types and Composition
Sanusi defines the complete cause and enumerates its possible compositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four possible configurations of the complete cause and how they differ based on agent type and effect composition.

Core Idea
The complete cause can be one cause or many, depending on the agent and effect.

Sanusi now turns to a finer point about the complete cause: it is not always a single thing. Depending on whether the agent is necessitating or free, and whether the effect is simple or composite, the complete cause can be made up of one, two, three, or four of the traditional causes. This passage lays out these possibilities clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four cases
Read the Arabic passage once through, then look at the translation. Try to pick out the four distinct scenarios Sanusi describes for the complete cause.
Notice that he starts with the simplest case and builds up complexity.
2
Distinguish between necessitating and free agents
The passage contrasts الفاعل الموجب (necessitating agent) and الفاعل المختار (free agent). Understand that a necessitating agent always produces the same effect, while a free agent chooses to act.
This difference affects which causes are needed for the complete cause.
3
Understand 'simple' vs 'composite' effects
A simple effect (بسيط) has no parts, whereas a composite effect (مركب) is made of parts. This determines how many causes are required.
For a simple effect, only efficient cause (or efficient + final) suffices.
4
Reflect on why possibility belongs to the effect
Sanusi notes that إمكان (possibility) is considered on the side of the effect, not the cause. This clarifies a common confusion about causation.
Think about why the cause itself doesn't need possibility built in.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإنها ) أي العلة التامة ( قد تكون علة فاعلية)، إما وحدها كالفاعل الموجب الذي صدر عنه بسيط، إذا لم يكن هناك شرط يعتبر وجوده، ولا مانع يعتبر عدمه، وأما إمكان الصادر فهو معتبر في جانب المعلول، ومن تتمته، فإنا إذا وجدنا ممكناً طلبنا علته . ( أو مع الغاية، كما في البسيط) الصادر عن المختار، ( وقد تكون مجتمعة من الأربع المذكورة ، ( كما في المركب ) الصادر عن المختار، وقد تكون مجتمعة من ثلاث منها كما في المركب الصادر عن الموجب.
His statement: (And it) i.e. the complete cause (may be an efficient cause), either alone like the necessitating agent from which a simple thing emanates, when there is no condition whose existence is considered, nor an obstacle whose absence is considered. As for the possibility of the emanated, it is considered in the side of the effect, and from its completion, for when we find a possible thing we seek its cause. (Or with the final cause, as in the simple) emanating from the free agent, (and it may be composed of the four mentioned, (as in the composite) emanating from the free agent, and it may be composed of three of them as in the composite emanating from the necessitating agent.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage defines the complete cause (العلة التامة) and enumerates its possible types. The author explains that a complete cause can be just an efficient cause (علة فاعلية) alone, as in the case of a necessitating agent (الفاعل الموجب) that produces a simple effect (بسيط) when no condition or obstacle is present. The possibility (الإمكان) of the effect is considered in the effect itself, not in the cause. Alternatively, the complete cause can be the efficient cause combined with the final cause (الغاية), as in a simple effect from a free agent (الفاعل المختار). It can also be composed of all four causes (efficient, material, formal, final) for a composite effect from a free agent, or composed of three (excluding one) for a composite effect from a necessitating agent. The passage follows an enumerative structure, listing four possibilities: 1) efficient cause alone, 2) efficient with final, 3) all four, 4) three of the four.

D) Five

Key Takeaway
The complete cause varies in composition based on the agent's mode (necessitating or free) and the effect's complexity (simple or composite).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you attribute events in your life to secondary causes, do you remember that the complete cause ultimately rests with Allah? How does this lesson change your perspective on relying on means?
?
The passage distinguishes between a necessitating agent and a free agent. In your daily actions, do you act as if you are a necessitating agent (compelled) or a free agent? What does that reveal about your tawheed?
?
If the complete cause can be just an efficient cause, what does that imply about your need to attach your heart to anything other than Allah? Are there things you treat as necessary causes that are not?

Objection on Possibility and Its Answer
The commentator addresses whether possibility must be considered in the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the view that possibility is not considered in the cause, by analyzing the nature of composite effects and the relational status of concepts like influence and need.

Core Idea
Possibility is a property of the effect, not the cause—the cause simply necessitates.

The objector tries to extend the consideration of possibility from effect to cause by analogy with material and formal causes. The commentator provides a nuanced answer, distinguishing between the parts of a composite and the composite itself, and explaining that concepts like influence and need are mere mental abstractions. This lesson unpacks the key arguments and clarifications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial answer
Read the first part where the objector argues that possibility should be considered in the cause, just like material and formal causes. Notice the answer: the true effect in a composite is the composition itself, not the parts.
The commentator cites al-Isharat for support.
2
Examine the further objection and answer
The objector raises difficulties with influence, need, absolute existence, and prior existence. The answer shows these are merely relational concepts abstracted by the intellect from the cause-effect relationship.
The commentator emphasizes these are not real external attributes.
3
Understand the obstacle clarification
The final part distinguishes between real obstacles (which may or may not exist) and hypothetical obstacles (which only imply hypothetical ordering). This reinforces that possibility is not a real attribute of the cause.
The commentator urges careful reflection, calling it subtle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومن تتمته فكأنه قيل ما يحتاج إليه الشيء الممكن في وجوده، فلا يعتبر في جانب العلة؛ لأن ما هو معتبر في المحتاج لا يعتبر في المحتاج إليه وما أورده عليه من أن اعتباره في جانب المعلول لا يقتضي عدم اعتباره في جانب العلة كالعلة المادية والصورية، فمدفوع بأن المعلول في المركب حقيقة هو التركيب والتأليف بين المادية والصورية، كما نص عليه في الإشارات، فلا يكونان معتبرين في جانب المعلول . قيل : إنه يشكل بالتأثير، والاحتياج، والوجود المطلق الزائد على الماهية التي هي نفس الوجود الخاص، والوجود السابق. والجواب : أنه ليس شيء منها مما يحتاج إليه المعلول بل هي أمور إضافية ينتزعها العقل من استتباع وجود العلة لوجود المعلول، وحكم العقل بأنه أمكن فاحتاج فأثر فيه الفاعل. فوجب وجوده فوجد، فإنما هي في الملاحظة العقلية، وليس في الخارج إلا المعلول الممكن، أو العلة الموجبة لوجوده، فتدبر فإنه دقيق. وأما رفع المانع فإن أريد به المانع في نفس الأمر فيجوز أن لا يكون فيه مانع ، وإن أريد به المانع الفرضي فإنما يستلزم الترتيب الفرضي لا في نفس الأمر.
His statement: (And from its completion) So it is as if it was said: What the possible thing needs in its existence is not considered in the side of the cause, because what is considered in the needy is not considered in the needed. And what was objected to him that its consideration in the side of the effect does not necessitate its non-consideration in the side of the cause, like the material and formal causes, is repelled by the fact that the effect in the composite is truly the composition and combination between the material and formal, as stated in al-Isharat, so they are not considered in the side of the effect. It was said: This is problematic with respect to influence, need, absolute existence superadded to essence which is the same as particular existence, and prior existence. The answer: None of these is something the effect needs; rather they are relational matters that the intellect extracts from the entailment of the existence of the cause for the existence of the effect, and the intellect's judgment that it was possible, so it needed, so the agent acted upon it. So its existence became necessary, so it existed. They are only in mental observation, and in external reality there is only the possible effect or the cause necessitating its existence. So reflect, for it is subtle. As for the removal of the obstacle, if by it is meant the obstacle in reality, it is possible that there be no obstacle; if by it is meant the hypothetical obstacle, it only entails hypothetical ordering, not in reality.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector argues: If possibility (إمكان) is considered in the effect, why is it not also considered in the cause, just as the material and formal causes are considered in both? The answer (مدفوع) explains that in a composite effect, the real effect is the composition (تركيب) itself, not the material and formal parts; thus they are not considered in the effect. Next, a further objection (قيل) raises difficulties with concepts like influence (تأثير), need (احتياج), absolute existence (وجود مطلق), and prior existence (وجود سابق). The answer (الجواب) clarifies that these are merely relational concepts (أمور إضافية) abstracted by the mind from the cause-effect relationship; externally, only the possible effect and its cause exist. Finally, the question of removing an obstacle (رفع المانع) is addressed: if the obstacle is real, its absence is possible; if hypothetical, it implies only a hypothetical order. The commentator urges careful reflection (فتدبر فإنه دقيق).

احتياج
وجود مطلق
وجود سابق
أمور إضافية
مانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if possibility is an attribute of the effect, it must also be an attribute of the cause, just as material and formal causes are in both.
Correction: The author shows that in a composite effect, the effect is the composition itself, so the material and formal causes are not considered in the effect. Similarly, possibility is not transferred to the cause.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, what is the true nature of the composite effect?
A) The material cause alone
B) The formal cause alone
C) The composition (تركيب) between material and formal
D) The efficient cause

Key Takeaway
Possibility is a property of the effect, not the cause; concepts like influence and need are merely mental abstractions from the cause-effect relationship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Impossibility of the Whole Being Prior to the Essence
An argument that the sum of matter and form cannot be prior to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the sum of matter and form cannot be prior to the essence itself, and how this relates to causality in composite beings.

Core Idea
The whole cannot be prior to the essence because they are identical.

In this passage, the commentator argues against the possibility of the composite whole (matter and form) being prior to the essence. Since the sum of parts is precisely the essence, claiming priority would entail self-priority, an impossibility. This lesson unpacks the logical reasoning and clarifies the distinction between conceptual and real priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to capture the author's argument. Notice the clear statement that the sum of matter and form is identical to the essence.
2
Identify the key premise
The passage asserts that the sum of material and formal parts is identical to the essence. This is the core premise that makes priority impossible. Understand why this leads to self-priority.
Think about what 'priority' means in this context.
3
Examine the objection and response
The text addresses a potential objection about the role of the complete cause (agent and end). See how the commentator distinguishes between the priority of the whole and the priority of the complete cause, which is allowed.
The complete cause is prior to the effect, but that does not make the whole prior to the essence.

His statement: ('let alone' with the addition of two other things) Its clarification is that if the essence is added to two things, it is prior to the composite of the essence and the two things with a self-subsistent priority, and if this composite is prior to the essence, then the essence is prior to itself by two degrees, which is more impossible than having priority over itself by one degree. Also, from the priority in the case of addition with the priority of a thing over itself follows the priority of the part over the whole, and undoubtedly the two corruptions are more serious than the one. And this is the meaning of his statement 'let alone etc.' His statement: (Because the distinction by generality and specificity is of no use) here because the discussion is about the priority of the sum of matter and form over the essence in reality, not in conception, and the mentioned distinction is not useful in that, but is useful in the priority or with the end is prior to the effect without doubt.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the impossibility of the whole (the sum of matter and form) being prior to the essence. The commentator begins by stating that the precedence of the cause over its effect is necessary, but when considering the whole as a whole, there is doubt. The reason is that the sum of material and formal parts is identical to the essence itself, so priority of the sum over the essence would be priority of a thing over itself, which is impossible. This is true even if we add two other factors (agent and end). The commentator then explains that the conceptual distinction between generality and specificity is irrelevant here, unlike in definitions. He concludes that if the complete cause is the agent alone or with the end, it is undoubtedly prior to the effect. The 'قوله' sections provide further clarifications: one clarifies that the effect in a composite is the combination, so matter and form are prior to that combination, not to the essence. Another addresses an objection that inherence and conjunction might be identical to the effect, but the answer distinguishes between dependence and necessary concomitant. The commentator emphasizes that the discussion is about real priority, not conceptual. He also notes that if the essence were added to two things, it would lead to priority by two degrees, which is even more absurd, and it would imply part prior to whole. Overall, the passage defends the position that the whole (matter+form) cannot be prior to the essence, while the complete cause (agent or agent+end) can be prior to the effect.

المادة
الصورة
العلة التامة
الفاعل
الغاية
التغاير الاعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conceptual distinctions (like considering the whole as a sum vs. as essence) can justify priority in reality.
Correction: The passage states that conceptual distinctions are ineffective for real priority; the sum and essence are identical in reality.

Checkpoint

B) Because matter and form are temporal, while essence is eternal.
C) Because the sum is always after the essence in existence.
D) Because the essence is simple and cannot have parts.

Key Takeaway
The sum of matter and form is identical to the essence, so it cannot be prior to it without implying self-priority.

The Condition as Part of the Agent
An objection that the condition is omitted from the division of causes and the defense that it is part of the agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition (shart) is not considered a separate external cause in Islamic kalam but is subsumed under the agent.

Core Idea
The condition is not a cause by itself; it is part of the agent's independent agency.

In the discussion of causality, an objector claims that the condition has been omitted from the division of external causes. The commentator defends the classification by arguing that the condition is part of the agent in reality. This lesson examines that objection and its resolution, clarifying the concept of the independent agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objected statement carefully. Understand why the objector thinks the condition is a missing division – it is needed for existence and is part of the complete cause.
2
Read the answer
Notice how the commentator redefines the agent to include the condition as a part. The condition is not independent but contributes to the agent's agency.
The agent is defined as the independent cause.
3
Reflect on independent agency
Ask yourself: How does this defense preserve the fourfold division? Consider that the condition is needed only secondarily through the agent.

وجزء أيضاً من العلة التامة هذا تأكيد لقوله فإنه من جملة ما يحتاج إليه الشيء في وجوده قيل ولك أن تحمله على التأسيس بناء على أن في لفظة أيضاً إشعاراً بأنه كسائر الأجزاء لا انحطاط له حتى لا يعتد به ولا
